Police brass defend officers against critics


Hearts
and soul
An artist's friends rally to
suDDOrt him and his art.


EDITOR'S NOTE: This is the first
in a three-part series on the opera-
tions of the Venice Police
Department following the critical
Matrix audit. Next: A detailed look
at some of the Venice crime statis-
tics VPD administration says show
cipe is way down in Venice.
BY TOMMY MCINTYRE
STAFF WRITER

Critical reports aside, the
.three top Venice police com-
mand officers said police are
doing a great job and deserve
more credit, and backed their
claims with statistics.
VPD Chief lim Hanks,
Deputy Chief Dan NMcGoogan
and Capt. Dave Dunaway
spoke with one voice after
requesting a 90-minute meet-


ing with a Ienice Gondolier
Sun reporter.
"We want citizens to know
that our officers are out there
working their hearts out to
serve the community," Hanks
said. "They need a pat on the
back and they are not getting
it in the media."
McGoogan also praised
the agency's 25 support per-
sonnel.
"They deserve a lot of cred-
it for their professionalism,"
he said. "because without
them. the officers couldn't do
their jobs."
There are a total of 58
sworn officers in the depart-
ment, including 15 supervi-
sors, and 83 total employees.
The department operates on
a $6.8 million dollar budget.


Not writing
enough tickets?
A recent Matrix Consulting
Group audit pointed out at
least 23 areas that Venice
police need improvement in.
One of those areas pur-
ported to show that VPD offi-
cers are not writing enough
tickets.
"That's just plain not true,"
Hanks said. "Look at the
numbers."
The yardstick used by law
enforcement agencies for
writing traffic citations is the
Traffic Enforcement Index
(TEl). The number is express-
ed as a ratio of tickets written
per traffic crash. The opti-
mumn number is 35-40 tickets
Please see POLICE, 6A

Nokomis revitalization moving forward


BYGERALDA. ROGOVIN
CORRESPONDENT
Approval by the Sarasota
County Planning Commis-
sion of a special exception for
the Nokomis Inn and Suites is
another step toward revital-
ization of Nokomis's com-
mercial corridor along U.S.
41. '
The zoning change will
permit the motel to add 27


FRONT SECTION


BUSINESS
DETERT EARNS AWARD
LEGALS
LET 'EM HAVE IT
. LOTTO .


rooms to its existing 21, raze
existing buildings on an abut-
ting tract of land and build
about 4,000 square feet: of
commercial and retail space.
The property is at 119 North
Tamiami Trail in Nokomis.
The next hurdle, following
the commission's 5-2 vote in
favor, is approval by the board
of county commissioners.
Dan Dragash, owner of the
Inn and developer of the


property, envisions starting
construction by next June, if
the BOCC approves by Jan-
uary. His plans also include
adding dormers and metal
roofs to the motel to provide
an old Florida-Key West
appearance. Existing and new
building will be painted pale
yellow, and shutters will be
Bahamas flamingo pink.

Land-use changes


questioned by


council members

Members want to know what future
developments will pop up with the increased
density in North Venice and other areas.


BY JJ.ANDREWS
ASSISTANT EDITOR


. Venice City Council mem-
bers were leery of approving
future land-use changes
Tuesday for hundreds of acres
of mostly undeveloped land
that could pave the way for
continued large-scale growth
in the North Venice area.
And when some on coun-
cil asked to know what would
be built there, city staff said
they couldn't say for sure
because of past actions by
council members.
This bothered Knights Trail
Road resident Dan Saiter,
who lives on acres of farm-
land in North Venice. Saiter
has done the math on maxi-
mum density, and told coun-
cil it risks destroying his par-
adise by opening the door to
allow more than 2.500 homes
to be built right next to his
property.,
The 60 acres of the Noko-
mis Groves/Kemerer along
Gene Green Road was desig-
nated for maximum density
at 18 units per acre, as was the
79 acres of Knights Trail Ex-
cavating.
Most of the land is unde-
veloped.
"It's like heaven out there,"
Saiter said to council. "It's
quiet. We've got animals that
live there, ..,. That (land-use
designation) is not compati-
ble with what we've got now.
"Yeah, we only have 17
homes in the area, but you
add 4,000, and it'll be a night-
mare. Please, please think
about some of these things.",
Unintended consequence
Previous land annexations
caused most of these re-
quests. Venice brought the
land into the city, meaning
definitions of land use must
match the city's comprehen-
sive plan, not the county's.
Future land-use changes
are no longer bundled with
the rezoning applications
based on those designations,
after council members com-
plained earlier this year they


felt like developers were rush-
ing projects past them.
This succeeded in slowing
down the process, but also
prevents council from know-
ing what will be built. Land
laws only require developers
to reveal their intentions dur-
ing rezoning.
Consequently, council
members do not know what
will be built along Knights
Trail Road in North Venice.
Despite many questions
from council members, the
changes to future land-use
designations for all but one -
Royal Capri were approved
and submitted to the state for
final approval by the De-
partment of Community Af-
fairs.
What will be built?
Land-use attorney Jeff
Boone, representing l&J
Homes, future developer of
the two properties, said there
are no development plans out
there for those 139 acres
along Gene Green Road.
While that may be true, the
property's 257-acre neighbor
to the south the former
Stay 'N Play Resort is own-
ed by the same company. l&J
Homes applied on Monday to
turn Stay 'N Play into the
Bella Venetia Community
Development.
All of this is piled on top of-
the development along Laur-
el Road east of Interstate 75,
where there could be more
than 5,000 new homes built.
Plans already are in the works
to expand the industrial cen-
ter, relocate two concrete
plants and build a 20-screen
movie theater and retail com-
plex--' all along Laurel Road.
Boone would not label the
impending residential devel-,
opments as being more
affordable, so blue-collar citi-
zens can buy them. He did,
however, say that higher den-
sity is needed in order to
lower housing costs.
"We need to find places in
all of Venice that can handle
Please see CHANGES, 6A

2A VENICE GONDOLIER SUN FRIDAY, NOV. 11,2005


A salute to the military

Venice Area Beautification Inc. would :
like to recognize and thank those who have
served or are currently serving to safeguard ,
all our loved ones and keep our country free.
To honor our heroes, Venice Area
Beautification's volunteers participate in the
maintenance of the Freedom Walk at Patriots
Park inVenetia Bay.
For those wishing to memorialize a
special person or all who have exhibited
heroism and leadership in preserving our
freedom, special opportunities, including
engraved payers, are available to be
displayed at the park. Contact Venice Area
Beautification; Inc. at 488-2236 or by
e-mail at vabioffice@venicechamber.comn.


The U.S. Postal Service
released the Distinguished
Marine Stamp Thursday on
the 230th birthday of the
Marine Corps. The
Distinguished Marine Stamp
honors four Marines: Gunnery
Sgt. John Basilone, Sgt. Maj.
Daniel J. Daly, Lt. Gen. John A.
Lejeune and Lt. Gen.Lewis B.
(Chesty) Puller. Basilone, also
known as "Manila John,"
fought on Guadalcanal with
the 1st Marine Division in
1942 and was awarded the
Medal of Honor for his hero-
ism. Daly is one of only two
Marines in history to receive
the nation's highest military
award, the Medal of Honor,
twice for separate acts of
heroism. Lejeune was the first
Marine to command an army
division and later became
commandant of the Marine
Corps, Puller, known affection-
ately by all his Marines as
"Chesty," was a battalion com-
mander and regimental com-
mander with the 1st Marine
Division during World War II
and the Korean War.

Charity event to raise money and spirits


BY CHRISTINA ELWELL
STAFF WRITER


Germaine Delaney has
high hopes for the Pure
Imagine Celebration and
Benefit that will be held on
Saturday for her partner of
seven years, David Landry.
"I think it's going to be a
pretty big event," Delaney
said. "I want to make sure it's
beautiful."
Landry is a local artist and
sculptor who has his work for
sale across the country and
has traveled all over the world
to show his creations. Busch,
Gardens, Selby-Botanical Gar-
dens and the Katherine But-
ler Gallery are just a few local
places where you can find
his unique carvings.
Landry has been diagnos-
ed with terminal pancreatic
cancer.
"A malignant tumor was
found in his portal vein and
the doctor was not able to
remove it," Delaney explain-
ed in an e-mail interview.
The way things were
As Landrys condition con-
tinues toq worsen, his busi-


ness, The Hearts Palm Gallery,
feels the effect. Germaine is
trying to help get things back
to the way they once were.
."David's been so sick for
so long and he hasn't been
able to work," Delaney said.
"Things used to be different."
Despite the fact that the
odds are against them, and
that on Thursday Landry was
too sick for an interview,
Delaney will not give up hope.
"The big question is how
long is he going to live, but
no one knows," she said.
"We hope he lives another 50
years.
"I'm not gonna give up
hope."
Delaney has chosen to
look back on happier times.
"We sometimes have too
much fun," she said. "Some-
times the carvings have
glasses or hats on them."
Delaney feels that this
benefit will serve to show
Landry how special and ap-
preciated he is.
"David is such an original.
I feel that he is a genius.
The creativity just pours out
of him," she said through
tears. "I started painting with


-. Y.

Con artists working scrub jay scam


'Developers' are trying to pick up land cheap
by telling property owners they can't build
on it because of the protected birds.


BY GEORGE MCGINN
STAFF WRITER


Con artists are using the
Florida scrub jay to scare
owners into selling their vac-
ant lands, says one county
official.
Sarasota County's Manager
of Resource Protection Amy
Meese said several landown-
ers have been contacted by
developers telling them their
land is "worthless" and offer-
ing to buy the land at far below
its current market value.
"Sarasota County wants to
reassure residents owning
land that may contain pro-
tected Florida scrub jay habi-
tat that their lots are not
valueless," Meese said.
Developers are telling land-
owners their property is un-


Start of a


buildable, she said.
"We're concerned because
we have become aware of
disreputable practices occur-
ring," Meese said. "We do not
want landowners in the com-
munity to be victimized."
Confusion over whether
lands identified as potential
scrub jay habitats, escalated
when the county updated its
Geographical Information
System maps in February
2005, based on data from the
U.S. Fish and Wildlife Service.
The GIS map identified two
zones: red and yellow. Red
zones are areas defined as
having potential scrub jay
habitat, and yellow zones
provide a 1,380-foot buffer
around the red zones.
"The current GIS map is
only an information guide to


new term


SUN PHOTO BY JJ. ANDREWS
Venice City Council members Rick Tacy, left, and Vice
Mayor John Moore are pictured at Wednesday's swear-
ing-in ceremony following Election Day. Tacy was re-
elected to his thirditerm, defeating challenger Gary Iy
Anderson 76.25 percent to 23.75 percent. Moore was
unopposed in his reelection bid and his name did not
appear on the ballot. Tacy and Moore both.thanked the
voters and complimented their fellow council members.
"Together, we've done a great job so far, and we just
have to continue doing it," Tacy said.


PUBLIC NOTICE
VENICE CITY COUNCIL

Notice is hereby given that the regularly scheduled meeting of the
Venice City Council to be held on November 22,2005, has been
canceled. The next regularly scheduled meeting will be held
December 13, 2005.
Lori Stelzer, MMC, City Clerk

and it is not a definitive iden-
tifier of where scrub jays are,"
Meese said.
In an earlier interview,
Meese said a red zone does
not mean that a scrub jay
actually lives there, just that
the habitat is favorable. How-
ever, that doesn't mean you
can't build on the lot.
For landowners looking to
build, Meese said an addi-
tional review step must be
followed when applying for a
building permit.
"The federal government
has (recently) advised us
(county) that there are no
unbuildable lots in Sarasota
County," Meese said.
Mitigation
A scrub jay survey needs to
be done by an approved envi-
ronmental consultant, Meese
said.
Land which is occupied by
the scrub jay may need a fed-
eral incidental-take permit to
clear property or build on it.
An incidental-take'permit
allows a non-federal land-
owner to proceed with an
activity that is legal in all re-
spects, but results in "inci-
dental taking" of a listed
species. This is allowed under
a mitigation process that
consists of:
Avoidance can the pro-
posed impacts be avoided by
revising the proposed devel-
opment? ,
Minimization if im-
pacts can't be avoided, can
the plans be revised to lead to
a minimal impact?.
Mitigation if impacts


can't be avoided or mini-
mized, the USFWS will
require compensatory miti-
gation to offset the impacts.
Mitigation fees are de-
signed to compensate for
impacts to protected species
and provide revenue for a
governmental agency to ac-
quire and manage replace-
ment. habitat for the benefit
of the species an applicant
will impact. Mitigation is
worked out on a case-by-
case basis with the USFWS.
Lots for sale
The city of North Port and
Sarasota County are poised to
sell about 2,100 North Port
residential lots that have been
abandoned by property own-
ers. Residents interested in
these: lots should become
knowledgeable about the
scrub jay habitat issue.'
Louis Fisher III of Fisher
Auction Co. Inc, held a meet-
ing Tuesday .to explain the
bidding process on these lots.
Fisher promised to explain
about the scrub jay habitat
when residents are inquiring
about the lots.
"There will be two phases
to the auction," said Fisher.
"The first will include online
bidding. Or, if people don't
have access to a computer,
they can come into the office.
"This first phase is design-
ed largely for individuals who
just want to buy one lot, like
the lot next door to their
house, for example."
"The second phase will be
for the sale of whatever re-
mains," he continued. "Lots
will be sold in groupings of

Biowaste found


at county landfill


STAFF REPORT


Four local hospitals have
been fined $2,500 each for
,improperly disposing of bio-
medical waste, a county offi-
cial said.
"There have been items
considered biomedical waste
found at the county landfill
on Knight's Trail Road," said
Sarasota County Biomedical
Program Waste Coordinator
Carolynn Branson. "Small
quantities of items like sharps
(needles) were found in clear
plastic bags."
The hospitals that were
fined were Sarasota Memorial
Hospital, Doctor's Hospital,
Venice Regional Medical Cen-
ter and Englewood General
Hospital, Branson said.
Branson said both used

VENICE GONDOLIER SUN 5A
FRIDAY, NOV. 11, 2005


School programs: How



do we know they work?


Detert honored


The school board considers a district proposal
to institute ongoing program evaluations.


BY GREG GILES
STAFF WRITER


School programs come and
go, often with their own pro-
tagonists. It's one reason why
it's so hard to evaluate their
success over time.
And successful programs
tend to "take on a life of their
own." In edu-speak that's
code for "supported and pro-
moted by the people who run
them."
For the past few years the
Sarasota County School Board
has been pushing for more
concrete evidence that key
programs are working, and
not just
because staff
who adminis-
ter them say
so. The board
is looking for
independent
analysis and l
evaluation of -
programs to i di
make sure Kovach
they're getting
the bang for the buck.
Options
On Tuesday Superinten-
dent Gary Norris and the dis-
trict's Research, Assessment
and Evaluation Department


presented a number of op-
tions to move forward with
evaluations.
Carlene Judge, executive
director of the department,
outlined three evaluation
models for the board to pon-
der.
The least
time-con-
s uming .
model would -"
focus on pro-
grams that .
may not be
meeting the
intended
need. Judge Norris
said high
school dropout prevention
programs and the alternative
school for young mothers,
Cyesis, are examples of pro-
grams currently under review
by the district.
Another option would tar-
get programs that have a dis-
trictwide impact. Examples
might be the use of data
coaches in schools, or profes-
sional learning communities
(teacher planning and self-
help groups).
A third, more comprehen-
sive approach, would be to
evaluate larger, new initia-
tives that have significant
financial implications and are


major organizational changes
in the way schools work.
Judge said the high school
reform contract with the
Institute for Research and
Reform in Education, NeXt
Generation Teachers and
NeXt Generation Learning,
and new technology set-ups
in new schools would fall
into this category.
Outside review
Evaluations of large-scale
programs, said Norris, need to
be conducted by an outside
agency or consultant, in part
due to the tremendous in-
vestment and resources
spent on those initiatives so
they can be continuously
monitored.
Norris said once the board
determines which way to pro-
ceed, he will develop a five-
year schedule of evaluations
so the board and staff would
be assured the evaluations
are going to take place.
Board member Frank
Kovach said he wanted pro-
grams to be evaluated based
on their cost to the district,
while John Lewis said he
supports prioritizing pro-
grams that affect the most
students. Both agreed outside
evaluation was preferable.
Board member Laura Ben-
son echoed that sentiment,
saying the district needs to
look at process as well.


PHOTO COURTESY OF NANCY DETERT
State Rep. Nancy Detert, R-Venice, tenter, was one of nine Florida House members recog-
nized Wednesday by Florida Legal Services Inc. as part of its Celebrate Access to Justice
event. Detert was chosen by FLS for her defense of mobile home owners and for her
efforts to secure affordable housing. Dorene Barker, FLS legislative director and staff
attorney, and Arthur Rosenberg, staff attorney, also participated in the ceremony.


"The old adage 'We've al-
ways done it.that way' needs
to go away," she said.,
Board chairwoman Kathy
Kleinlein noted there would
be costs associated with
what would undoubtedly
be time-consuming evalua-
tions. She suggested a posi-
tion might need to be creat-
ed for a program evaluation


coordinator.
Judge said she would
come up with a list of priority
programs to evaluate, based
:on comments presented by
board members at Tuesday's
workshop.

POLICE from Page A


CHANGING LAND USES
Future land-use designations decide what a property can be rezoned to. Here are the most recent approvals by Venice City Council, which still must be approved by the Florida
Department of Community Affairs.
If the DCA rejects any of these requests, then developers cannot apply for this type of zoning:
* APAC/Rinker Materials of Florida Nearly 10 acres on the north side of Gene Green Road, 900 feet east of Knights Trail Road; change future use from "rural" to "industrial."
Rezoning to industrial is pending before city council to allow a 5,000-square-foot facility be built to provide 30 new jobs by the end of 2006, according to Rinker attorney
Michael Furen.
* APAC, Venice Minerals, Miami Valley Ready Mix and CEMEX Nearly 279 acres in the northeast quadrant of the city, north of Laurel and Knights Trail, via access Gene
Green Road; change future use from "rural" to "industrial, commercial."
* Country Club Condominiums About 1.13 acres at the corner of Guild, Fairway and Golf drives; change future use from "low-density residential" at less than 2 units an acre
to "medium-density residential' at 14-18 units per acre. Site plans have already been approved, and construction on four, two-story buildings housing a total of 16 condomini-
ums called Villa Veneto, owned by Renaissance Group of Venice, will start upon DCA approval.
* Fiore Di Venezia Five acres on East Venice Avenue, west of Auburn Road; change future use from "medium-density residential" at 5-9.9 units an acre to "moderate-density
residential" to 6-13 units per acre. Rezoning plans have already been approved for a 38-unit condominium development, pending DCA approval.
* In-Island Development About 1.8 acres at 437 Nokomis Ave.; change future use from "office, professional and institutional" to "medium-density residential" at 14-18 units
per acre. A rezoning application, on hold by the city, would replace the vacant nursing home with 26 townhouses, a pool and clubhouse.
* Knights Trail Excavating Nearly 79 acres on Knights Trail Road, north of Laurel Interstate Business Center and Caribbean Bay Club; change future use from "rural" at 1
unit per 5 acres to 'medium-density residential' at 14-18 units per acre. No rezoning request on file, but a maximum of 1,422 living units could be built here with this zoning.
* Nokomis Groves/Kemerer Sixty acres on Knights Trail Road, north of Laurel Interstate Business Center and Caribbean Bay Club; change future use from "rural" at 1 unit
per 5 acres to "medium-density residential" at 14-18 units per acre. No rezoning request on file, but a maximum of 1,080 living units could be built here with this zoning.
* Royal Capri Council denied approval on this 40 acres on the east side of Pinebrook Road, south of Laurel and north of Kilpatrick roads. Owners were asking to change the
land from Sarasota County's "moderate-density residential" at 2-5 units per acre to the city's definition of "moderate-density residential," which would allow 6-13 units per acre.
The Waterford Homeowners Association had about 200 residents show up to Tuesday's council meeting in protest of Royal Capri.
* San Lino Twenty acres at the southeast corner of Auburn Road and Venice Avenue; change future use from "medium-density residential" at 5-9.9 units an acre to "moder-
ate-density residential" at 6-13 units an acre. Site plans have already been approved, and construction on Waterford's 132 condominiums on what was formerly known as the
Kunze Property will start with DCA approval.
* Stephenson Property About 0.71 acres at the corner of Guild and Fairway Drive; change future use from "moderate-density residential" at 2-4.9 units an acre to "medium-
density residential" at 14-18 units an acre. No rezoning request on file, but a maximum of 12 living units could be built here with this zoning.
* Villa Lago About 46 acres at on Pinebrook Road, just south of Laurel Road; change future use from "moderate-density residential" at 2-4.9 units an acre to "low-density res-
idential" at less than 5 units an acre. The rezoning application has been on hold pending revisions after opposition from neighbors.
* VICA About 399 acres at at 2662 Border Road extending north to Laurel Road; change future use from "major employment center" to "moderate-density residential" at 6-
13 units per acre. No rezoning request on file, but the developer's attorney has indicated that 2,700 living units could be built here.

Way above standards

tre important because Matrix
reported that VPD had only a'

our corrected numbers, -it
shows our ratio to be well
above the TEI standard at
1:103."

another improvement that
began before the Matrix
audit.
Staffing. McGoogan said
the department is coordinat-
ing with the city manager's
office to determine the best
use of personnel.

Dragash has been "the
poster child for what we're
trying to accomplish along,
U.S. 41 in Nokomis," John
Ask, president of the Nokomis
Area Civic Association, told
the planning commission.
"But we need others to follow
his lead. The business com-
munity has to step up to revi-
talize this blighted area." :
Kick start


The $32.4 million project
that will widen.U.S. 41 to six
lanes between the Venice
Bypass and Laurel Road, and
improve the existing highway
from Laurel to State Road 681,
may have been a catalyst for
revitalization. But, as Ask and
Bruce Dillon, chair of the
Nokomis Revitalization Com-
rnirtee, have pointed out, the
enterprise needed a strong
first effort to kick start


improving the. community's
commercial corridor.
Dragash's motel is the only
one in the vicinity, despite the
proximity of Nokomis Beach
and other tourist attractions.
Conversion to condomini-
ums has eliminated several
others. The most recent was
Addy's Inn in Nokomis, on the
east side of U.S. 41.

FRIDAY. NOV. 11. 2005


SAFE from Page 1 A
Principal Candace Milling-
ton said Tuesday she wanted to
dispel the rumor, and reassure
parents and students that VHS
honors and advanced place-
ment courses would not only
remain intact, but increase in
the coming years.
"We are in a process of a
program review,, which was
just initiated. This seems to be
causing some anxiety," Mill-
ington said.
Assurances
"I want to assure the stu-
dent body and the communi-
ty our honors classes and AP
will be maintained," Milling-
ton said. 'At this time we see
no reason to reduce those
offerings. We are not going to
move backward. We are going
to move forward."
If anything, Millington
hopes to increase the number
of advanced courses. The
school has tripled their en-
rollment in the last five years,
expanding from three to 14
AP courses, according to the
school.
More than 400 AP tests
were administered last year,
double that of five years ago.
Based on a student's perfor-
mance, college credit is
awarded.
VHS has honors programs
in all disciplines. Many stu-
Y'dents are able to leave VHS
with from three to 15, college
'credits.
Millington attributes the
program's success to block
-'scheduling, which provided
more time for more advanced
,course offerings.
"We are very proud of
that," Millington said.
Getting to know you
IRRE staff suggested the
school enhance its honors pro-
gram by providing the lead
teacher an additional period off
to monitor enrollment and to
i encourage other students to
i take higher level courses, ac-
cording to Millington.


"They want more students
to take rigorous courses,"
Millington said.
'"A student who wants or
needs an honors course
throughout the day ... we are
going to make sure they have
the opportunity to do that."
"IRRE staff are still in the
process of learning about us,
just as we are learning about
them and finding out ways to
best partner together. We are
all interested in best prac-
tices," Millington said.
Confusion
Laurie Levin, director of
JIRE, confirmed she received a
number of angry e-mails from
Venice High students, some
containing offensive language.
Levin called it a misunder-
standing over a discussion
about equity.
IRRE staff spoke to teach-
ers two weeks ago about cre-
ating equity throughout the
learning environment. IRRE
consultants spoke about an
educational environment
where all students have the.
same program opportunities
and where student learning
communities reflect the goals
and desires of the student
population.
"I think some peopleheard
that all the advanced courses
would disappear, and that
every student would be in
every kind of class. Neither is
true," Levin said.
"There is a basic misun-
derstanding about the names
of things with the things
themselves. The names of
things will be different, 1but
the things themselves will
remain," Levin said. "What
people heardwas some of the
designations would not be
there, and therefore the class-
es would disappear. That's not
the case. We are hoping to
expand honors classes to a lot
more students who are capa-
ble of doing the work. We
dream of the day when all
classes are honors classes."

OUR VIEW



Con men capitalizing on scrub jay concerns


arasota County has issued a warning
that con men are operating in North
Port, preying on land owners, some who
live out of town.
A press release (posted on the county Web
site) claims some North Port developers are
contacting property owners with letters or
are calling on the phone, telling people that
their lots are unbuildablee" because the lots
have scrub jay habitat on them.
The developers are said to be trying to buy
these lots "beneath current market value."-
Considering current prices, it appears they
may be trying to buy lots for pennies on the
dollar.
There's a grain of truth in what these
"developers" are saying: It can take a year or
more to get a permit from the federal govern-
ment, and it may be necessary to spend a
great deal of money for what is called "miti-
gation" if the lots are deemed scrub jay habi-


tat. But they're not unbuildablee."
The timing is interesting because the
county (and the city) are about to auction off
2,200 lots lost by the owners who failed to pay
taxes. Officials estimate that the county and
city could pocket as much as $60 million.
Earlier, the county had warned a substan-
tial amount of North Port's vacant property is
scrub jay habitat. The scrub jay, a small bird
with blue feathers is only found in Florida. It
is protected by the federal endangered
species act as it has lost its habitat as land is
developed.
Sarasota County Commissioner Jon Thax-
ton suggested recentlyitwas up to the potential
buyer to do his or her "due diligence" before
purchasing one of the 2,200 North Port lots.
The lots are to be put up for sale on the
Internet. People may be bidding from other
parts of the country and from as far away as
Europe. Most will trust Sarasota County and


North Port to be be honest about the proper-
ty. It wasn't that many years ago that Florida
got a worldwide black eye for letting devel-
opers sell swamp land on TV
The county press release suggests that
North Port "lots are not valueless, despite the
claims that their location within (scrub jay)
habitat areas" make the land unbuildablee."
Amy Meese, the county manager of re-
source protection said: "The county and city
want residents to know that they are being
misled," adding "we don't want landowners
in the communityto be victimized."
The release continues: "The federal gov-
ernment has advised us that there are no
unbuildable lots in Sarasota County."
A federal fish and game supervising biolo-
gist said people can build on scrub jay lots,
but are required to pay for two additional lots
as "mitigation." So a person bidding $40,000
on a lot might end up paying $120,000 if


there is scrub jay family nearby. And it also
could take time to obtain the required per-
mits from the federal government to build.
The federal Fish and Game Department
says on its Web site: "Because it is difficult to
predict the location or status of Florida scrub
jays, we are advising property owners to con-
tact this office (in Vero Beach) at least one
year prior to building on their property."
If an individual builds in violation of the
Endangered Species Act, that owner could
spend a year in jail and pay a $100,000 fine.
Corporations pay $200,000.
Larry Arnold, the county official who
heads up the team working on the tax lot
sale, says he understands the concern.
Arnold believes the county has an ethical
obligation to explain potential problems
with the lots to out-of-state buyers. ,
He promised he will see that the scrub jay
issue is made very clear.


Speaking out:


Moore and Tacy


Two candidates for city
:.Qouncil recently appeared,be-
fore the Venice Area Demo-
cratic Club. Although the
nonpartisan election is over,
council member John
Moore's and Rick Tacy's com-
ments show the depth of their
understanding of the city and
the amount of work entailed.
Gary Anderson did not show
up.
Here are some of their
comments abridged and
sometimes paraphrased be-
cause of space limitations -
beginning with an opening
statement.
Speaking of his three years
on city council, Moore said
"This experience is one I
would not have traded for
anything. I love the job .... I
have a work ethic and I sup-
pose it is pathological with
me that I delve into it as much
as possible."
He then explained future
work would include the U.S.
41 Bypass, revitalizing the
Seaboard District, increasing
the balance of our commer-
cial light industry, and airport
and land development issues.
Tacy, who has been on
council since 2000, men-
tioned he has never missed a
council meeting or workshop.
He helped settle the airport
issues with the FAA, secured
height restrictions near the
beach and helped correct the
Venice Housing Authority's
problem.
He sees the Venice down-
town; beach and airport as a
three-legged stool that should
be cared for in order to the
keep the city in good shape.
Q. What is your position on
height restrictions?
Tacy: I have been strug-
.gling with that right now. I
am not completely sold on
"35 feet and that's it." The city
should have restricted
heights. Seaboard could be
higher, but not competing
with the water tower, a city
landmark.
Moore: Height is a moving
target. You can't deal with
height unless you deal with
density, permitted uses, loca-
tion, etc. Laurel and 75 is dif-
ferent from downtown
Venice.
Q. According to a recent sur-
vey, more than half of Sarasota
County residents favor spend-


ing county funds on affordable
housing. Recently, led by coun-
cil member Tacy, council voted
to support affordable housing
by leasing land' to Habitat for
Humanity. It will build 20
homes. What is the next step
Venice should take in making
more affordable housing?
Moore: This is an extreme-
ly important issue. I sit on
the Manasota League of
Cities, which is an advocacy
group to the Florida
Legislature. Affordable hous-
ing is one of our objectives.
The Sarasta land trust
can be utilized n Venice. In-
clusionary zoning-- 10 to 15
percent affordable housing
for 20 percent greater density
- is fine for the county but
questionable for the city.
Mixed use apartments and
commercial could be used
in the Seaboard area. If it is
affordable, so be it.
Tacy: Affordable housing is
limited as to where it can go.
Most of the city is close to
built out or laid out. There
may be about 4,000 acres we
can annex out by Knights
Trail and perhaps can get
some housing out there.
It is best to work with the
county to keep their program
going.
The housing authority is
planning to add 60 units for
those who earn 60 percent or
less of the county's median
income.
Q. Some people feel that the
heavy financing of candidates
chosen by the closed group
Committee for Quality Govern-
ment discourages others from
running and does not make for
a true democracy. There is also
concern that the members may
unduly influence the candidate
after the election. Others feel
that the best candidates are
chosen this way. Your opinion?
Tacy: I'll be right up front.
I have already accepted
money from the PAC. I don't
believe the PAC money buys
the elections. The Good Old
Boys love.this community as
much as anyone .... Others
can form PACs that might
take an opposing view.
The PAC helped introduce
Judge Moore to the commu-
nity. You will see Judge
Moore's actions and my
actions have not always gone
the way the CQG would have
liked.
Moore: Three years ago
the CQG agreed to support
me.... My theory is to know
who the constituents are. I
want to know who is in the
Venice Taxpayers League, the
Neighborhoods Coalition,
the chamber of commerce,,
the CQG. I want to know all
these people, talk with them.,
Giving a little money for a
campaign frankly isn't going

Please see COCCARO, 9A

LETTERS FROM OUR READERS


If we play ball, so can our kids


Editor:
It's months before Little League baseball starts at Chuck
Reiter field in Venice. Sure, there's Fall Ball and AAU, but the
boys (kids) of summer are months away from the familiar call
of "Play ball."
This is a good thing because the fields at both Venice facili-
ties (Wellfield, Chuck Reiter) are in desperate need of repair.
Weeds and red ants are threatening to take over the infields.
Scoreboards absent of bulbs sit on rusting poles needing paint.
I, like many fathers or mothers whose sons or daughters
play ball, try to volunteer time or dollars to fix an infrastructure
that has long been capitalized by concession stand sales and
increasing registration fees..
Most people are aware of the valuable lessons our children
learn by playing organized sports: teamwork, the thrill of victo-
ry and the agony of defeat. How to win with class and to lose
with dignity. Practicing skills to improve one's abilities.
Learning that hard work and effort are often rewarded, but
sometimes not.
If nothing else, keeping our children focused and busy
keeps them out of trouble.
Just like a team, if the community would work together and
give just a little, we could have programs and resources our
community would be proud and where our children and
grandchildren would have the opportunity to hear the familiar
words "Play ball!"
Should a child or parent contact you or your business
requesting donations or sponsorship for Little League, please
take a moment and give.
Let's help give all the children of Venice, Nokomis and
Osprey the opportunity to play in a first-class facility.
Matt Brower
Venice


Epiphany is spending
its funds on its mission

Editor:
Please allow me to clear up
the misunderstanding about
Epiphany and their blessed
pilgrimage to Ireland this past
July.
All of the fortunate travel-
ers that converged in New
York and on to Ireland paid
their own way. Some were not
actually Epiphany parish-
ioners, but all "were touched
deeply by a God who truly
loves us," as Father Michael
Cannon would say.


The state of Epiphany is
very much God loving, but
not without financial chal-
lenges, as are many charities
these days.
Most of us have increased
our donations to this blessed
congregation in order to
replace the leaking roof, the
obsolete air conditioning and
various maintenance needs.
The school has been sup-
plemented by the parish for
years. In order to "control the.
bleeding," additional funds
are required of those who are
able to pay tuition; thus a
long-overdue, dreaded tu-


ition increase has indeed
been levied.
We have a small founda-
tion set up for the school, but
it lacks the core funding to
supplement much of the
tuition and cover the true cost
for those paying and those
unable to\pay. Many of our
travelers and parishioners
have sent in donations specif-
ically to the Epiphany Foun-
dation that supplement
school tuition in perpetuity.
This is a spectacular school
and church community that
houses the cathedral for our
entire Venice Diocese.
May the love and work of
Epiphany's tremendous staff,
religious and lay persons
alike, as well as the thousands
of involved parishioners, be
recognized as the beacon of
Venice that it truly is.
God bless us everyone!
Paula Knott
Nokomis
Wants more information
on school referendum
Editor: '
I would like to offer my
appreciation to the Venice
Gondolier Sun for its recent
two-part series on the drop-
out rate in Sarasota County.
Of course, there are many
other concerns in public edu-
cation other then what is the
correct information on drop-
outs. My question remains:
How did the taxpayers and
students of this local school
district benefit from the
passed 2002 four-year refer-
endum that is said to be gen-
erating more than $100 mil-
lion for this school district?
Yes, they tell u's about lots
of new hiring, higher salaries,
programs,, etc. But how have


our students progressively
benefited since 2002 in higher
GPAs, higher FCAT and SAT
scores? Just how much has
the achievement gap been
closed?
Until the proponents of the
new referendum can prove to
voters how the extra dollars
given to the Landings has
really helped the students, I
fail to see how voters will
want to buy into the many
new promises, including a
very costly plan called NeXt
Generation Learning.
The bottom line? If there is
not an clear and truly inde-
pendent analysis, including
Department of Education
involvement, I fail to see why
any already overburdened
county property taxpayer
would vote for renewal, what-
ever the millage rate.
And, of course, if they real-
ly need the'extra dollars, why
canit the state provide it? And
what has happened to all
those many billions in lottery
funding for education?
Many questions.
Norm Lupo
Venice

A grandmother waits and worries


MARY KAY RUPPEL
GUEST COLUMNIST


Dear Sean,
I've e-mailed your ship, but
have not heard back from
you, so I don't know where
you are at this time. Your dad
says you're out at sea some-
where, but we don't know
where, and I especially want-
ed to contact you in honor of
Veterans Day.
I want to say "thanks" to
you and your shipmates for
your efforts inseeing that we
at home can celebrate anoth-
er peaceful Veterans Day. :
My special thanks to you
and all the guys on your sub-
marine who were present on
March 19,2003, in the Persian
Gulf for the initiation of
''Operation Iraqi Freedom," I
was so worried knowing that
you were in the midst of the
invasion and knowing your
sub was involved in sending


off those missiles.
Please know that the
American people appreciate
what you're doing, and try to
ignore the negativism that
you guys read about in this
country.
Sean, did you know that
Veterans Day was originally
Armistice Day? Nov. 11. is the
anniversary of the armistice
that was signed with the
Germans in 1918 so the hos-
tilities of World War I ended. I
think that the' words of
President Woodrow Wilson in
1919 concerning Armistice
Day are very relevant to Vet-
erans Day in 2005. He said:
"To us in America, the re-
flections of Armistice Day will
be filled with solemn pride in
the heroism of those who
died in the country's service.
and with our gratitude for the
victory, both because of the
thing from which it has freed
and because of the op-
portunity it has given Amer-
ica to- show her sympathies
with peace and justice in the,
councils of the nations."
. On May 24, 1954, because
many new veterans had little
or no association with World
War I, President Dwight D.
Eisenhower approved the
changing the name of the
Nov. 11 celebration to "Veter-
ans Day."


It has always been a very
important day to me as we
have had a lot of servicemen
in our family.
To start with, there was
your great-great-grandfather,
who won a Congressional
Medal of Honor in the Civil
War. The commendation
(which hangs in your dad's
house) states that he had put
his life in danger to rescue a
.fallen comrade.
My dad, your great-grand-
father served in the U.S. Army
duringWorldWar I; my broth-
er was a naval pilot in World
War II; and your grandpa and
dad both/served in the U.S.
Marine Corps.
So,' as you can'rsee, you
have followed in the brave
footsteps of a family that has
fought and served to preserve
our peace and that of other
nations.
So, dear grandson, let me
say. how very proud of you I
am and that I pray each day
that God will keep you safe
and that we will-be together
very soon.
Love, Grandmary.
Mary Kay Ruppel is a
I .nice resident and has been
a frequent contributor to
other publications. She will
be writing on a regular basis
for the Gondolier Sun.

When do the locals get a break?


Don't need them. Once again another comment on the
snowbirds. They should not be given any cut or financial break
on city services or any taxes. I do appreciate that the snowbirds
come here, but as time goes on we can realize that without the
snowbirds, we will be just fine. So we need to stop bending over
and kissing their butts every time they come in town. How
about those who live here and work year-round to make this
city available for when the snowbirds do come in? Come on,
appreciate what we've got: We don't need the snowbirds.


Nobody stops. I read Sun-
day's paper about stop signs.
There's one on the comer of
Golden Beach and Spadaro.
Nobody stops there. Someday
there's going to be a terrible
accident there.
Need chairs. There is a new
airport terminal in Fort Myers.
It would be nice if there were
chairs for the seniors to sit
down on to take their shoes off.
Seal the border. The situa-
tion on the U.S.-Mexico bor-
der is out of control. After
Sept. 11, we cannot tolerate a
porous border. It must be
sealed. It is a long-term prob-
lem and needs a long-term
solution. I have one. We will
always 'need basic training


camps for our military. I pro-
pose we build a string of them
from the southern Texas bor-
der to the Pacific coast. They
would be spaced about 250
miles apart. Troops engaged
in basic training would go out
on patrols 24 hours a day as


part of their desert training.
The patrols would ren-
dezvous half way between the
basic training camps. This
would eliminate the enor-
mous expense of the immi-
gration service. We could train
our recruits and secure our
border, all for the same dollar.
Too small. Being a cross-
word puzzle aficionado, I am
very disappointed in the
Friday morning edition of the
Gondolier Sun because it is
too small, you've got all the
empty space around, expand
the puzzle so those of us with
fading eyesight can work it.

11 Critical Home Inspection Traps
You Should Know About
Before You Put Your Home Up For Sale


Sarasota County,- According to industry
experts, there are over 33 physical problems
that will come under scrutiny during a home
inspection when your home is for sale. Anew
report has been prepared % which identifies the
eleven most common of these problems, and
what you should know about them before you
list your home for sale.
Whether you own an old home or a
brand new one, there are a number of things
that canfall short of requirements during a
home inspection. If not identified and dealt
v with. any of these 11 items could cost you
dearly in terms of repair. That's why it's:
critical that you read this report before you list
yourhome. If you wait until the building
inspector flags these issues for you. you will
.almost certainly experience costly delays in
the close of your home sale or. worse, tutr
prospective buyers away altogether.
,l ,,npnl krt l


In most cases, you can make a
reasonable pre-inspection yourself if you
know what you're looking for, and knowing
what you're looking for can help you
prevent little problems from growing into-
costly and unmanageable ones.
To help homesellers deal with this
issue before their home is'listed, a free
report entitled "11 Things You Need to. Know
to Pass Your Home Inspection" has been
compiled which explains the issues
involved.
To hear a brief recorded message
about how to, order your free copy of this
report, call 1-866-214-7091 and enter ID
7003. You can call anytime, 24 hours a
day, 7 days a week. Call NOW to learn
hpw to ensure a home inspection doesn't
cost you the sale of your home.

PGT turns 25


BY CHRISTINA ELWELL
STAFF WRITER

. Progressive Glass Technol-
ogy celebrated its 25-year
anniversary Oct. 27.
Founded as Vinyl-Tech in
1980, the company's first
product, the EZE Breeze, was
sketched on a napkin by
founder Paul Hostetler.
In 1987 it manufactured its
first line of glass windows and
became PGT.
Today, PGT has a 725,000-
square-foot facility and gen-
erates revenue of more than
$250 million a year selling
windows from the coast of the
Bahamas to Australia.
"We've come a long way
since we started," said Kirsten
Pedersen, public relations


support.
PGT has about 2,500
employees here and at its
other plants; many of them
have been with the company
a decade or more.
"I've been here since
March of 1989," Jose Santiago
said. "I'm hoping to be here
for another 25 years.
Santiago began his career
with PGT working in the
warehouse and today is the
international sales manager.
"There's definitely room
for advancement," Pedersen
said.
Celebration
At the anniversary celebra-
tion, employees were invited
to view a specially made
museum of PGT history, re-


ceive a collectible PGT mini-
truck, and have their fill of
cake.
"We created the museum
just for the employees," Pe-
dersen said.
One of the items on display
from the PGT time capsule
was a vent from PGT's very
first order. The piece even had
the original vinyl.
"This is something the
company was founded on,"
Pedersen said.
Other items in the muse-
um were newspaper articles
about PGT from the 1980s,
PGT merchandise and old
photos of employees.

You can e-mail Christina
Elwell at: celwell
@venicegondolier.com.

Charleston Place to adjoin Casey, s Tavern


ROGER BUTTON
BUSINEWS


Three well-known Venice
businessmen -- Paul
Hostetler and Bill Morse of
Triple Diamond Commerce
Plaza, together with James
Lombard, who developed
Venetia Bay Center are
putting together another ex-
citing project for Venice.
Charleston Place will be
located on 6 acres south of
the Merrill Lynch building,
opposite Casey's Grill & Tav-
ern on Venetia Bay Boulevard,
U.S. 41 Bypass.
Manhattan Construction
of Tampa will begin building
the 95-residence community
later this year. Phase 1
includes 36 of the one- and
two-bedroom condomini-
ums and townhouses 'priced
from $400,000. ,
There will be lap and social
pools, and a fitness center,
catering area and game room
in the 2,500-square-foot
meeting house with parking
under the buildings.
With no golf course or
water frontage, the develop-
ment based, upon appealing
Charleston, S.C., architecture,
will have an urban living,
pedestrian-oriented theme.
Michael Saunders & Co.-
will sell the properties.
Sponsor a tree angel
Customers can call or go
by the remodeled Blessings
Thrift Store, 425 East Laurel
Road in Nokomis and place


their bids for the colorful,
brightly decorated 9-foot
Christmas tree before Dec.J12,
said store manager Carmen
Lanzo.
Together with her prede-'
cessor, Linda Karminsld, she
invites everyone to partici-
pate in their Sponsor A Needy
Child with a Toy campaign.
Blessings supports the.
Victory Baptist Church, Burn-
ey Road, off Old Venice Road
in Osprey, overseas and local.
programs.
Sunday school teachers
and pupils will make the tree
angel, globe 'and bell orna-
ments, each having the name
of a needy child in this com-
munity and of the church.
Customers pick any of the 45
children's names to give a
new, unwrapped present.,
dropping it off at the store or
the'church.
"If you cannot sponsor a
child, we have a jar here into
whichyou can drop a quarter,
a dollar whatever you can
afford and we will use that
to fill in the gaps the tree
angels don't cover," said
Lanzo.
Blessings has already set
up its complete Christmas
display in the big window of
the store and welcomes
donations. For bids, call 483-
3969
Bradways opening
on the Trail
It is celebration time again
for the Bradways as their new
business just keeps on grow-,
ing.
Richard and Linda Brad-
way, together with their 30
agents, are moving from
Nokomis Avenue, where they
shared offices with colleagues
at Roberts Insurance. This
weekend they Will open their
new offices at 230, South
Tamiami Trail on the island,
two blocks south of Venice
Avenue.


"We're so excited to have
our own building just the way
we want it," said Linda.
They are just entering their
second year, second time
around, in the completely-
renovated offices with state-
of-the-art equipment.
Office condos launched
Opportunities to take the
first step in business are avail-
able at jacaranda Commerce
Center close to McDonald's at
the 1-75 Jacaranda Boulevard
Interchange.
Merritt Realty has released
eight new office condos rang-
ing from 1,200-1,600 square
feet in the yellow, Key West-
style building at 375 Com-
mercial Court.
"Everyone wants owner-
ship, not leasing," said broker
Terry Purdy. "Construction
costs have jumped in the past
few months, but for the initial
launch these will be $275 per
square foot."
$2 million acquisition
The Seaboard Executive
Center, 312 E. Venice Ave., a 1-
acre lot, was acquired by
Watch Tower Trading LLC
'from Donald G. Lowry for $2
million recently.
According to MaryBeth
Wilson of ERA at 221 W.
Miami Ave., the new owners
intend to continue to operate
it as is...
There are 35' executive
offices in the 11,000-square-
foot, two-story building with
an additional 35 small ware-
houses.
"Where else can you get an
office for $400 monthly and a
warehouse for $225? It's a
unique, one-of-a-kind, one-
stop workplace which over
the years has assisted many
small businesses grow, in-
cluding attorneys, appraisers,
Realtors and a church," said
Wilson.
Barbara Baseman of Mi-


chael Saunders & Co. also
handled the transaction.
Calusa Park activities
Construction vehicles ap-
peared on the new Calusa
Park property at Laurel Road
by Mission Valley Boulevard
this week.
Developer Robert Man-
sell, managing member of
MTH Associates, reports it
will be early next year before
the real work begins, as per-
mitting still has to 'be com-
'pleted.
The old house on the prop-
erty is being readied for the
sales staff, who are moving
from Gondola Park on Capri
Isles Boulevard, as it will be
completed this month.
The first stage of Calusa
Park is sold out, and Mansell
said, "I am not selling any
more at this stage because of
the current market condi-
tions."
Plans were announced by
BUSINEWS 12 months ago
for 120 luxury condomini-
ums, a recreational club-
house with gymnasium and
swimming pool on this for-
mer Toplihe Horse Farm
property.
La Paloma open house
'The newly improved La
Paloma Assisted Living Fa-
cility at 800 East Baffin Drive
in South Venice opens its
doors to the public 5:30-7:30
pm., Friday,. Nov. 18. Owners
David and Kimberley Gris-
som have completed renova-
tions to the six-bed facility
with new carpeting and deco-
rations.
Commercial sales slowing
Are commercial buyers
walking away from deals be-
cause of the moratorium in-
troduced by Sarasota County
commissioners? ReMax Real-
tor George Huhn says they
are because they are not pre-


pared to wait six to nine
months for acceptance and
processing of applications for
rezoning site plans or devel-
opment.
He is aware of 15 deals un-
der contract that have been
canceled.
"It's continuing the overall
slowdown in the commercial
market, and if it happens in
other districts it could have
serious effects," said Huhn.
Brian Meurs, president of
the Venice Area Board of
Realtors and a'ReMax Realtor,
sees the situation differently
and does not think it will have
a detrimental effect on the
market as a whole.
"It's a very specific area and
only a six-month moratori-
um. Sarasota County com-
missioners are looking long
range for future generations
at what is the best for the
community," said Meurs.
"Building is most important
to the community bringing in
good jobs and there has to be


commercial land available for
companies to expand."
Annexation sought
A small property of 1.4
acres behind the Magnolia
Condominium project under
construction by Waterford
Companies on Albee Farm
Road was recently acquired
by Patrick and Barbara
faehne.
The small enclave adjoining
Bay Indies in the middle of the
city limits needed to be
annexed into the city limits,
according to Jaehne. who
added, "We will probably do
something with it in the future;
it could be residential.'
The city report indicates it is
targeted for incorporation and
impact fees will be charged on
any future dwelling unit.

Stephanie D. Feloney
Stephanie D. Feloney of
Venice died Tuesday, Nov. 8,
2005. She was 73.
She was born April 22,
1932, in Center Rutland, Vt.,
and moved to the area in
1994.
She graduated from the
Fanny Allen School of Nurs-
ing and retired in 1992.
Survivors include her hus-
band of 52 years, James; a
son, Terrance of Huntington,
Vt.; a daughter, Patricia of
Venice; two sisters,, Jane of
Rutland and Sophie of Strat-
ford, Conn.; and several
nieces and nephews. .
Services: A mass will be held
May 20, 2006, at 11 a.m. at St.
Eugene's Catholic Church in
Wells River, with burial.to follow
in West Newbury, Vt. Lemon Bay
Funeral Home is in charge of
arrangements.
Contributions: Memorialidona-
tions may be made to TideWell
Hospice and Palliative Care, 5955
Rand Blvd., Sarasota, 34238.

many years.

Church.

sons, Gerald of Venice, and

grandchildren; and seven
great-grandchildren..


Alan J. Schmidt
Alan J. Schmidt of
Venice died Saturday,
Nov. 5, 2005. He was
78.
He was born July 22, 1927,
in Munroe, Mich., and moved
to Venice in 1995 from Chi-
cago, III.
He graduated from Val-
pariso University and served in
the U.S. Army Medical Corp.
He retired from Sunbeam
Corp. after 25 years and then
became an independent
business owner.
He was a member of the
Independent Manufactures
Representative Association,
Waterford Tennis Club and
South County Investment
Club.
He belonged to St. John
Lutheran Church inWheaton,
Ill., and Lakeside Lutheran
Church of Venice.
Survivors include his wife of
55 years, Joan; a daughter,
Carol Guerra of West Chicago,
Ill.; three sons, David ofVenice,
Stephen of Wheaton and
Gregory of West Chicago; a sis-
ter, Haroldene Wagner of Clear-
water;, eight grandchildren;
and one great-grandchild.
Services: Services will be Friday,
Nov. 11, at 11 a.m. at Lakeside
Lutheran Church.

tions may be made to Lakeside
Lutheran Church, 2401 South.

Elmer J. Whalen
Elmer J. Whalen of
Venice died Saturday,
Nov. 5, 2005. He was
89.
He was born March 18,
1916, in Riverton, Ill., and
moved to Venice in 1991 from
Columbus, Ohio. He was a
certified public accountant
and cofounder of the ac-
counting firm of Whalen &
Co. He was a member of the
Public Accounting Society of
Ohio.
He was a U.S. Navy veteran
of World War II; 'a 32-degree
Mason and member of Linden
Lodge 637, Columbus; Scottish
Rite, Lodge Council Chapter
Consistory, Valley of Colum-
bus; and a founder of Kiwanis
Little League Softball.
Survivors include a daugh-
ter,, Maggie Dahl of Venice;
two sons, Jim of Bradenton,
and Jedd of Venice and Co-
lumbus; six grandchildren;
and 12 great-grandchildren.

Columbus with interment in
Blenden Township Cemetery,
Westerville, Ohio. Ewing Funeral
Home is in charge of arrange-
ments.

tions may be made to the Amer-
ican Diabetes Association,

Eisenhower Blvd., Suite 295,
Tampa, 33634.

FRIDAY
NOV. 11, 2005


Harriers

gear up for

Saturday's

finale

BY CLAUDE LEWIS
SPORTS EDITOR


Little Everglades Ranch in
Dade City will be overrun by
runners Saturday as the
FHSAA will hold its Cross
Country Finals for Class 1A
up through 4A.
There will be races for girls
and boys in each class. Ac-
tion begins at 8 a.m. with 1A
and will progress up to 4A at
noon.
Venice High will be repre-
sented in both the girls and
boys 3A races.
The Venice girls, coached
by Brenda Clark, qualified as
a team by placing fourth in
last Saturday's Region 3 Meet
at Lakewood Ranch. The top
six from each region qualified
for the state meet. -
Qualifying as an individual
for the 3A Boys Finals is Kyle
Brunette, who was 11th in the
regional running. The top 15
individuals from each region
qualified for state.
The girls 3A race will start
at 10 a.m. and the boys will
follow at 10:30 a.m.
Dade City is in Pasco
County.
The Lady Indians will try
to improve on their times'
and turn in a respectable per-
formance against the state's
elite.
The girls pre-race seeding
has 173 runners listed. Of
those, the top Venice runner
is Stephanie Korszen, who is
54th with her region time of
21 minutes, 26.70 seconds.
Chelsea Rose is 59th with
21:40.70. Genna Jones is
122nd at 23:21. Erin Mc-
Cullough is 134th in 23:43.
Alex Natiss is 156th in 24:36.
Katie Pfost is 158th in 24:45.
The top seed in the girls
race is Kelly Parrish of Van-
guard-Ocala. Her time is a
sizzling 17:50.
On the boys side, Brunette
is seeded 68th out of 182
runners with his time of 17:55.
The lowest boys time is 15:52
by Jon Blocker of Lawton
Chiles.

Lady booters
edge North Port

STAFF REPORT

The Venice High girls vars-
ity soccer team upped its
record to 3-0 with a 2-1 vic-
tory over visiting North Port
High Tuesday at the Gar-
brandt Soccer Complex.
Venice broke on the score-
board first when Kara Ferretti
scored on a direct free kick
from 25 yards out.
It stayed 1-0 at the half.
Ferretti found the back of
the net again 23 minutes into
the second half. She took a
pass from Kristen Johnson,
drove to the comer, then
drove to the net.
North Port scored with five
minutes remaining, ruining
Meghann Dunn's shutout bid.
The Venice junior varsity
defeated North Port 6-0.
Ashley Ojeda 'and Colleen
Kennedy had two goals
apiece.
The Venice junior varsity
and varsity played Thursday
night at Lakewood Ranch.
Girls hoop Mooney
The Venice High girls vars-
ity basketball team won its
preseason opener Tuesday
night, bouncing visiting Card-
inal Mooney, 46-33. Samantha
Worthington led the way with
13 points while Andrea
Krankowski added 12 points.
50 plus basketball
league forming
A senior basketball Jeague
is forming. For more informa-
tion, contact the Sarasota
County Call Center at 861-
5000 and ask about the over-
50 and over-60 basketball
leagues forming at Woodmere
Park.

Lady Indians back



in title match


BY CLAUDE LEWIS
SPORTS EDITOR

The Venice High volleyball
team is back in familiar terri-
tory.
Perhaps the Lady Indians
can finish the deal this time
around.
Playing business-like ball
like a team on a mission,
Venice dispatched of West
Boca Raton Thursday night
in the FHSAA Class 5A semi-
final at The Lakeland Center.
It took three games in just
over an hour to catapult the
Lady Indians into Friday's 7
p.m. State Championship
game against Tallahassee-


Lawton Chiles.
Venice (25-7) won 25-18,
25-23 and 25-12 over West
Boca to advance against
Chiles (28-3), which defeated
Lake Howell in three straight.
Venice returns for another
title match. Last year, the
Lady Indians had to settle
for second after losing to St.
Thomas Aquinas in five
memorable games.
There are only a handful
back from that team, but
they all played pivotol roles.
Middle blocker Dana Dumas
was the big gun with 19 kills.
Outside hitter Natalie Gau-
dreau added 10 kills and 12
digs. Setter Nickie Halbert

Indians start playoffs with unbeaten Green Wave


BY CLAUDE LEWIS
SPORTS EDITOR


The Venice High football
team has played tough com-
petition all season long.
Why would it be any dif-
ferent in the regional play-
offs?
,The Indians, 3-7 and run-
nerup in District 11, will be
decided underdogs when
they travel to Fort Myers,
10-0 and District 12 champs,
in Friday night's Class 5A
Region opener at Edison
Stadium.
"There are 32 teams that
have a chance," said Venice
coach Nick Coleman. "We're
all 0-0."
Having played the likes of
Lakeland, Booker, Manatee,
Pace and Gulliver Prep,
Venice knows what it takes.
"We've had a tough sched-
ule," he said. "We've seen the
best. We've played with the
best. We'll do our best to


contain them."
Coleman calls Fort Myers
a very tough squad that is
solid in all aspects.
"They're more of a run-
ning team," Coleman said.
"They'll lull you to sleep."
The Green Wave's wing-T'
running game is led by 5-
foot, 6-inch Jamaul Temple
(No. 3), who has gone for
1,045 yards and has scored
14 touchdowns. The other
half is bruising Sergio Rivera
(No. 33), who has amassed
845 yards and has tallied 17
TDs.
"They're cagey," Coleman
said. "They run hard between
the tackles."
Quarterback Tyce Havens
has thrown for 653 yards.
Jeffery Banks, also a stand-
out defensive end who leads
the team in tackles and
sacks, has 228 receiving
yards. Rivera is also a threat
to catch the ball out of the
backfield.


"We can't give up the big
play," Coleman said.
Venice's offense has done
a good job moving the ball
the past few games. The line
has been executing and
Tony Charles and Kiki Mer-
zius have been running hard.
Quarterback Matt Wick-
wire is also a threat running
and flipping the ball to a
back. Once in a while, he goes
deep to the likes of Austin
Carter and Tyler Moses.
Coleman said Fort Myers
has strong linebackers and
comers. He added that the
Wave's special teams are
super.
"They have an unbeliev-
able kicker," he said of Greg
Yankopoulos, who has 41
extra points and three field
goals.
Venice and Fort Myers
have one common oppon-
ent. Fort Myers defeated
Charlotte 28-7 and Venice
edged the Tarpons 20-13.

Girls golfers in 7th heaven


Place seventh at
FHSAA Class 2A Finals
in Vero Beach.

BY CLAUDE LEWIS
SPORTS EDITOR

If just being at the FHSAA
Class 2A Golf Finals was like
heaven for the young Venice
High girls team, then placing
seventh out of 16 schools at
the prestigious event played
in Vero Beach had to feel like
seventh heaven.
The two-day tournament
held at The Club at Pointe
West wrapped up Wednesday.
The Lady Indians shot a
team score of 346 on the
opening day Tuesday, leaving
them in seventh place. They,


like most every other team in
the tourney, scored a little
higher Wednesday. The Venice
girls were four strokes higher
at 350.
The total score of 696 left
the Lady Indians seventh,
sandwiched between sixth-
place Manatee at 684 and
eighth-place Niceville at 698.
Even champion Lake
Howell was up a little 302-'
307-609.
Steady Casey Kennedy led
Venice scorers with her 77-
80-157. Fellow freshman
Crystal Smith was next at 80-
81-161. A double bogey on
the final hole Wednesday had
her feeling a bit down.
Junior Megan Clipse
showed the most improve-
ment from day one to day
two. She shaved 10 strokes


off her 98 score on Tuesday.
She fired an 88 Wednesday
for a tourney total of 186.
Senior Lindsey Hinshaw
closed out her VHS career
with a 94-101-195. Although
she was upset at her second
day score, she has been a
valuable performer for the
program the past four years.
Sophomore Katie Kennedy
finished with a 95-101-196.
"It was a great experience
for them," said co-coach
Larry Sandburg, who handled
the reins in Vero while Shari
Lindsey was away at LPGA
school. "The girls were dis-
appointed in themselves that
they didn't do better, but I
don't think they understand
what they accomplished.
They shot pretty well."
The greens were slower
Wednesday, resulting in put-
ting woes for a few of the.
Venice girls. Smith could have.

round Wednesday.

Winged Sprints
back at CCS
Saturday night will see an
avalanche of racing at Char-
lotte County Speedway.
For one, the Tampa Bay
Area Racing Association's
Winged Sprints will run their
29th and final race of the
2005 season. The points title
will be up for grabs. Dave
Steele is still hanging on by a
thread over Keith Butler.

will run its second points
race. Two have been canceled
because of rain.
Charlotte County Speed-
way's Sportsman cars will go
50 laps.
Also on the schedule are
Super Stocks, Thunder
Trucks, Road Warriors and 4
Cylinders.
Racing begins at 7 p.m.
Charlotte County Speed-
way is located off Exit. 161 of
1-75 on Piper Road in Punta
Gorda.

Legion's Hunt golf.

The eighth annual Lenny
Hunt Memorial Golf
Tournament will be held
Saturday, Nov. 19, at Lake
Venice Golf Club.
This tournament funds the
various charitable causes that
Venice No-Vel American
Legion Post 159 supports.
There are two options for


those wanting to join in the
fun. Folks can play for the
Legion Championship or play.
in a scramble.
The Legion Championship
will be decided by low gross.
Only Legionnaires, SAL and
auxiliary members are eligi-
'ble for the championship.
There will be flights for men
under 50, under 60, under 70
and over 70. For women,
there will be flights under 60
and 60 and over.
The first three teams in the
". ..."


scramble will receive tro-
phies.
There will be closest to the
pin, longest drive and putting
contests.
The tournament will have
an 8 a.m. shotgun start.
Following the tournament at
2:30 p.m., there will be free
beer' and ,a baked chicken
lunch at the Legion Hall
under the bridge on the
island. To get in on the tour-
nament, call either' 485-4748
or 497-1050.

His motel opened in the

were added in the 1960s, and
six more were built in 1989.
Since Dragash's purchase
three years ago, he has ac-
quired an adjoining parcel
about 2 acres in area.
"I could have torn down
the motel, and put up 36 con-,
dos in the space quite com-
fortably," he said "But that
won't serve this market to its
best advantage..
"There were weeks last
winter during which I had to
turn away as many as 20 vaca-
tioning families because we
were booked to capacity," he


recalled. "There's an awful lot
of demand for rental space
year-round here."

PUBLIC HEARING
NOTICE OF INTENT TO CONSIDER THE ADOPTION OF
CITY OF VENICE, ORDINANCE NO. 2005.50
NOTICE is hereby given that the City Council of the City of
Venice. Florida will hold a public hearing beginning at 1:30 p m.
or shortly thereafter, on December 13. 2005. in Council Cham.
bers, City Hall, 401 West Venice Avenue, Venice, Florida, to con-
sider and act upon the adoption of the following proposed City
Ordinance:'
AN ORDINANCE OF THE CITY OF VENICE, FLORIDA, AMENDING'
THE CODE OF ORDINANCES, CHAPTER 74, UTILITIES, ARTICLE
I, IN GENERAL. SECTION 74-9(b) REFUNDS,'TO CHANGE THE
AMOUNT OF TIME A UTILITY CUSTOMER DEPOSIT IS HELD; AND
PROVIDING AN EFFECTIVE DATE.
Purpose of Ordinance: Amending Code of Ordinances Chapter
74, Utilities to reduce the length of tire that the city holds a util-
ity customer's deposit.
This notice is published pursuant to the requirements of Section
166.041. Florida Statutes; accordingly. the publication of same
must be accomplished at least ten (10) days prior to the meet-
Ing at which the above Ordinance is to be considered and acted
upon. A complete draft of the proposed Ordinance is on file In
the Office of the City Clerk for inspection by the public between
the hours of 8 a.m. to 4 p.m., Monday through Friday.
This public hearing may be continued from time to time.,
No stenographic record by a certified court reporter is made of
this meeting. Accordingly, any person who may seek to appeal
any decision Involving tie matters noticed herein will be respon-
sible for making a verbatim record of the testimony and evi-
dence at this meeting upon which any appeal is to be based
All interested persons are Invited to attend and be heard. Writ-
ten comment filed with the City Clerk of the City of Venice, will'
be heard and considered.
If you are disabled and need assistance, please contact the City
Clerk's office at least 24 hours prior to the meeting.
/S/
Lori Stelzer, MMC, City Clerk
PUBLISH: November 11, 2005

PUBLIC HEARING
NOTICE OF INTENT TO CONSIDER THE ADOPTION OF
CITY OF VENICE ORDINANCE NO 2005-49
Please be notified that the City Council of the City of Venice.
Florida at its regular meeting on the 131h day of December,
2005, in City Hall. 401 West Venice Avenue. Venice. Florida at
1:30 p.m.. or shortly thereafter, will consider and act upon the
adoption of the following proposed Ordinance:
AN ORDINANCE OF THE CITY OF VENICE, FLORIDA, AMENDING
THE FUTURE LAND USE MAP OF THE COMPREHENSIVE PLAN
FOR THE CITY OF VENICE TO CHANGE THE DESIGNATION OF
APAC/RINKER MATERIALS OF FLORIDA INC. PROPERTY FROM
"RURAL" TO "INDUSTRIAL"; AND-PROVIDING AN EFFECTIVE
DATE.
Purpose of Ordinance: To amend the Future Land Use Map for
property as follows:
The 9.9 acre metes and bounds property lying within Section
22, Township 38 South, Range 19 East, Sarasota County, Flori-
da. Located on the north side of Gene 'Green Road approxi-
mately 900 feet east of the intersection of Gene Green Road
and Knights Trail Road, North Venice, Florida, shall be desig-
nated as "Industrial".
This notice is published pursuant to the requirements of Section
166.041, Florida Statutes; accordingly, the publication of same
must be accomplished at least ten (10) days prior to the meet-
ing at which the above Ordinance is to be considered and acted
upon. A complete draft of the proposed Ordinance is on file in
the Office of the City Clerk for inspection by the public between
the hours of 8:00 a.m. to 4:00 p.m., Monday through Friday.
This public hearing may be continued from time to time.
No stenographic record by a certified court reporter is made of
this meeting. Accordingly, any person who may seek to appeal
any decision Involving the matters noticed herein 'ill be respon-
sible for making a verbatim record of the testimony and evi-
dence at this meeting upon which any appeal Is to be based.
All Interested persons are invited to attend and.be heard. Writ-
ten comment filed with the City Clerk at the above address, will
be heard and considered.
If you are disabled and need assistance, please contact the City
Clerk's office at least 24 hours prior to the meeting.
/s/ Lori Stelzer, MMC, City Clerk

JACARANDA LIBRARY AT HALLOWEEN 16B


WWII recognized at






County History Center


STORY AND PHOTOS BY JEREMY ROTTTGEN STAFF WRITER

ases, Beaches and Bond Drives exhibit
revisits Sarasota County during World
War II.


Finding materials for the
recent exhibit at the Sarasota
History Center wasn't hard for
archivist Mark Smith.
"I don't see anybody doing
the jitterbug around here,"
Smith said. "But it just gives
you the idea of what this area
was like during World War II."
Gathering materials from
residents and other organiza-
tions like the Venice Historical
Society, the exhibit shows
what was happening in all of
the county's areas during
World War II.
"This was a big boom for,
Sarasota County," Smith said.
"'You might say that people
rediscovered us. We had all
these troops here. I.mean
Sarasota had been pretty
stagnant for 'years. With two
bases here there were thou-
sands of men and women
coming down here. It was a
huge boom, for the area.
People rediscovered. A lot of
the people who came here
during the war came back
later to retire."

ATA GLANCE
Who. Sarasota County Htistory

Wh it.Bases, Blahes ahd.. i
iodlDnves .. .. --
When Exhibit is.onr view until
Dec: 30 I "
'Where: 701 North Tamiami
Trail. .
-HIow'much: Free
Hours: 10 a.m-4 p.m.
Monday-Thurisday, 9a.m.-1
p.m. Saturdays-; closed holiday
weekends.

When Smith spoke with
many of the residents who
loaned items, many of them
said that WW,\I gave Sarasota
its jiunpstart..
"Especially Venice," Smith
said. ."Because Venice was
went to sleep almost after the
Brotherhood of Locomotive
Engineers left. Except for Dr.
Albee and the KMI, Venice
was a sleepy little town, so I
would say Venice really got a
junpstart. It was a wonderful
place for the Army to go to
because there were so many
empty buildings for them to
use."
Land was cheap around
here in those days and wide


open. Rebounding from the
Great Depression, Sarasota
was fortunate to have the
bases come here. In 1940 the
city of Sarasota had 11,141
residents and the city. of
Venice had 507.
Maps of Venice during
World War II show the Army
bases and a PO.W. camp that
housed 200. There were
PO.W. camps all over Florida
during the war. '
Bombs away
, Maps and aerial photos
show) the target. ranges found
in North Port and Longboat
Key during the war. Both hot
properties now, but just right
for target practice about 60
years ago.
The Venice Army Air Base
was created as a training cen-
ter in 1942 by the federal gov-
ernment, with the entrance
located on San Marco Drive.
Service groups, units respon-
sible for airplane mainte-
nance and supply, were the
primary occupants of the
facility. Fighter: Squadrons
were transferred to the base
for training of combat pilots
and ground crew. An Army
Band was organized on loca-
tion. The Florida Medical
Center was taken over by the
Army Medical Corps and ren-
ovated for use as a military
hospital. The base ceased all
operations in November
1945.
James V. Morrow
James V Morrow, 84, do-
nated his Marine Corps
"greens" and his father's
World )War I uniform. He
enlisted in Sarasota in 1916.
"I enlisted in the Marine
Corps at the old federal build-
ing in downtown Sarasota in
1942," Morrow said. "My
mother and my sister had liv-
ed here for years and years."
Morrow had just married in
January 1942. The couple
decided to come down to Sara-
sota to see Morrow's mother.
Morrow made the decision to
sign up because the odds were
pretty good that he would be
drafted anyway.
"I figured they'd (each me
how to take care of myself
better than anybody else.
would," Morrow said: "Be-
sides that, they had the best'


Morrow with his mother by the waterfront in Sarasota.


This aviator's mask is one of the pieces on display at the center.


looking uniforms."
No sooner than he signed
up, Morrow was shipped off.
He was 22 years old.,
"That was the last time my
wife saw me for six months,"
he said. "They hauled me
straight to Parris Island from
Orlando."
Morrow's mother lived in a
cottage on Oak Street in
Sarasota and he had lived in
Sarasota and worked at a gro-
cery store.
Morrow wasn't the only
one in his family to join the,
service. His great-grandfather
served under Gen. Sherman .
in the Civil War and his father
fought in WWI. From the age
of 10 until he finished high
school, Morrow's step-grand-
mother took care of him with
a Civil War pension of $45,a
month in Bellefontaine, Ohio.
Morrow spent about three
\'ears in the states with his
wife at Camp Lejeune. He
attained five stripes in that
time, which was highly un-
usual in the Marine Corps in
that short of a time. Morrow
was then shipped off to. the
west coast. Because of his
stripes, Morrow was a gun-
nery sergeant when he board-
ed the USS Bonhomme Rich-
ard for Hawaii.
"Because the captain of the
Bonhonmme, Richard decided
to break the speed record
between San Diego and Pearl
Harbor, they buckled two
plates on the forward end of
the ship and dropped .me off
in Hawaii while they fixed the
ship. One of my old students
from aviation supply school, a
first lieutenant, saw me out
there picking up cigarette
butts or something probably
and he said 'Sergeant
Morrow, what are you doing
out here?' I said I'm getting
ready to go to Iwo Jima. He
said, 'Like hell you are. You're
staying here.' The rest of those
guys went off and I doubt if


James V. Morrow with his wife Katherine today.


any of them ever came back. I
could've ,kissed that lieu-
tenant on both cheeks."
During his time in the
Marine Corps Morrow never


heard a gun shot in anger.'
Morrow has his own real
estate office on Tampa Ave-
nue in Venice. He is also pres-
ident of the board for Valencia


Apartments.
"It was the best education I
ever had," Morrow said of his

Divas wanted for Venetian Harn


STAFF REPORT


SUN PHOTO BY FRAN VALENCIC,
From left Bob Sullenberger, Harry Brown and George
Heldmann taste some of the delicious food at the Knights
of Columbus installation. This trio volunteers at Knights of
Columbus bingo afternoon and at the monthly breakfast
fundraisers.


Venetian Harmony Chor-
us, which performs through-
out the greater Venice area,
wants local divas to join the
fun. "Even if you don't think of
yourself as a diva, you can
come find the diva in you,"
said Nancy Nlanville, mem-
bership chair. She assures
women who visit that they
will "make great music and
great friends at rehearsals of
Venetian Harmony every
Monday evening. The chorus
meets at the United Church


of Christ, 620 Shamrock Blvd.,
Venice. '"And for a few hours,
women can become a diva,
before heading back to their
everyday lives," Manville con-
tinued.
The award-winning cho-
rus performs for clubs, con-
ferences and celebrations
from Bradenton to Punta
Gorda. "They perform ballads
and up-beat tunes, with
choreography that adds spe-
cial pizzazz;" said Manville.
'All this singing and perform-
ing can bring out the diva in
anywoman," Manville added.


nony Chorus

The current divas of Ve-
netian Harmony are prepar-
ing for holiday performances
and a show for their fans
and the entire community,
Sunday, Feb. 19, at the North
Port Performing Arts Center.
The chorus won first-
place medals in their cate-
gory at Regional Sweet
Adelines competition in each
of the last three years. They
also placed fourth overall,
competing with choruses
large and small from
throughout Florida.
