THE LOCAL SCENE NOV. 11 NOV. 12


This is Marine photographer Lou LoweKy's picture of
the first flag-raising on Iwo Jima on the morning of
Feb. 23, 1945. In the foreground holding a carbine is
Pfc. James Michels; behind him, Cpl. Charles Lindberg;
to their left, Lt. Harold Schrier, who holds the flagpole
as Sgt. Ernest Thomas looks on. Holding the pole on the
other side is Sgt. Harry Hansen and behind him, with a
radio on his back, Pfc. Louis Charlo.

An independent study re-
leased. last week named
Sarasota Memorial Health
*. ~ - Care Sys-tem as one of the
S.* -* Top 100 Cardiovascular Hos-,
- -- *. petals in the nation.
The local hospital was
ri a .spotlighted in today's Mod-
I I -. em Healthcare magazine,
- - along with results of an an-
it nual study by Evanston, Ill.-
based Solucient that objec-
s Providers" tively measures the care
heart patients receive in the
* - nation's hospitals for heart
--- -4 attacks and cardiac proce-.
^ dures such as bypass grafts
and angioplasties .- and
.-- identifies those demonstrat-
- ing superior outcomesand
_- a. performance on key criteria.
- - ,It is the fourth consecutive
* - 8- year that Sarasota Memorial
- o has received Solucient's 100
-- Top Cardiac Hospital recog-
nition. No other hospital in
* Manatee or Sarasota County
*- -- made the nation's top list.
- Earlier this year, Sarasota
Memorial also ranked No.
19th for cardiac care/cardiac
D -- .- surgery (and top .50 in six
-, - other specialties) in U.S.
. -- News & World Report's an-
--* nual "Best Hospitals" study
published in July."
"The consistency of con-
-- secutive annual awards is a
- testament to the caliber of
physicians, nurses and staff
caring for our patients, and


to the hospital board's com-
mitment to providing, our
community with the highest
quality health care," said
Sara-sota Memorial Hospital
CEO Gwen MacKenzie.
The Solucient 100 Top
Cardiovascular Hospitals
study. reveals that top-per-
forming facilities consist-
ently outperform their peers,
especially in terms of mortal-
ity and 'complication rates.
In short, top hospitals had
lower rates of death and com-
plications, were able to send
their patients home earlier
and had average lower costs
than comparable. facilities.
Among other findings, Solu-
cient's study revealed:

If peer hospitals (non-
winners) provided the same
quality of care as the 100
Top Hospitals, survival rates.
would increase by more than
10,000 patients each year.
Complications of care
could also decrease in peer
hospitals. More than 1,100
additional patients could be
complication-free.
Among top community,
hospitals, post-operative in-
fection rates were an average
42 percent lower than infec-
tion rates in (non-winning)
peer hospitals.
The 2005 winners includ-
ed 30 teaching hospitals
with cardiovascular residency
programs; 40 teaching hos-


- .


* Medicare Part 0 EnroIlmet
* Global Pharmacy Access
* Free. Meds Programs
* American Generics


pitals without cardiovascular
residency programs; and 30
community hospitals (in-
cluding Sarasota Memorial).
For a list of the Solucient 100
Top Hospitals: Cardiovascu-
lar Benchmarks for Success.
winners and- a summary of
the report, visit http://www.
100TopHospitals.com.
"By identifying hospitals
that display superior perfor-
mance in the clinical man-
agement of cardiovascular
disease, we offer the health
care industry a direction for
positive change and infor-
mation to help decrease the
variations in quality and
financial performance that
continue to plague health-.
care today," the study reports.
Statewide, SMH has the
second largest cardiac pro-
gram (volume of open-heart
surgeries) in Florida and
offers the most advanced
diagnostics in the region,
including cardiac catheteri-
zation, intracoronary ultra-
sound, transesophageal
echocardiography (TEE),
cardiac CT angiography,
stress testing, nuclear imag-
ing, the latest MRI (magnetic
resonance imaging) and the
area's only electrophysiology
(EP) program. Non-surgical
procedures include angio-
plasty and stenting, including
drug-eluting stents, and,
brachytherapy for bypass
grafts narrowed by plaque
development. A cardiac re-
habilitation program, and
heart failure and anti-coagu-
lation disease management
clinics ensure heart patients
have access to the full con-
tinuum of cardiac care.

SOUTH NEWS


Pancakes, spaghetti


on the menus


ELLEN HILLSTROM
COLUMNIST


The activities of the South
Venice Civic Association real-
ly increase when our winter
residents return home. They
'come back with great -enthu-
siasm and want to do things
and have fun.
So, coming up:
The public is invited to
join the Beach Access Com-
mittee tomorrow, Saturday,
Nov. 12, from 8to 11a.m. for a
pancake breakfast. There will
be scrambled eggs, sausage,
applesauce, juice and coffee.
for only $5. All the net pro-
ceeds from the breakfast ben-
efit the operations of the ferry
and boat ramp. Maybe they'll
even give you a one-day free
ferry pass as well.
The following Friday, Nov.
18, from 4 to 7 p.m., you are
also invited to join the SVCA
members at Italian Night.
Bring your family and friends


over for fun and enjoy a good
old-fashioned family-style
spaghetti-and-meatball-sup-
per for $6 per person. Feel free
to BYOB.
Both activities are held at
the South Venice Community
Center, 720 Alligator Drive,
South Venice.
Memberships .and .ferry
passes may now be pur-
chased for 2006, giving you
almost 14 months, to Dec. 31,
2006.
Ask for details by calling
493-0006, or see us at either of
the above events.
Advance notice: The SVCA
will be wrapping up-its 50th
Anniversary celebration with
a holiday party on Friday,
Dec. 9. Tickets are $18 per
person. It is a catered affair by
Buffalo Bill's Catering Serv-
ices.
I am pleased to announce
that Eric Watters, Venice's fa-
mous song-and-dance man,
will be our entertainer at the
event, back by popular de-
mand. Make reservations
early as this will be a quick
sellout.
Please honor all veterans
on this Veterans Day 2005.
Ellen Hillstrom is the
South Venice Civic
Association development
director. She writes a regular
column for this page.

BY ANNE KLOCKENKEMPER
STAFF WRITER-
Greg Triebe, petty officer
third class in the 24th Seabee
Reserve Unit, recently re-
turned home from a six-
month stint in Iraq. It was his
third trip overseas, after previ-
ous tours in Bahrain and
Kuwait.
"I'm just happy to be
home," he said.
Triebe, 32, arrived in North
Port on Oct. 14 to the relief of
his parents, Al and Maureen,
who have been North Port
residents since 1981. Al-
though both her husband and
eldest son also served in the
U.S. Navy, Maureen never
became accustomed to her
son being gone.
"The worry is always
there," Maureen said. "He
was on active duty for 10
years, got out and got into the
reserves knowing that this
could happen, and it hap-
pened. But we're proud of
him."
While Triebe was overseas,
he served as a contractor and
was stationed at a military
base that was formerly under
the command of Saddam
Hussein. Unlike some bases,
Triebe's was safe enough that
he didn't have to wear his flak
jacket or carry his weapon at
all times.
'"All that stuff together was
about 40 pounds," Triebe
said. "We were fortunate
where we were."
During Triebe's absence,


SUN PHOTO BY KHARLI ROSE
Maureen Triebe shares a light moment with son Greg, back in North Port after a six-month tour


of duty in Iraq.
the family relied heavily on
Veterans of Foreign Wars Post
8203 for support.
"It's the absolute best. I
can't stress it enough," said
Maureen. "Al was in the mili-
tary and my older son was in
the military, all my brothers,
and my father but never at
a time like this. The VFW,
especially this one here, no
matter what I needed if I
needed support, if I had any
questions all I had to do
was call."
Pat Love, commander of
the VFW post, held a "wel-
come home" party for Triebe
and his family at the VFW hall
on the night he flew in. He has
also continued to make him-
self available to the family

TOOTHACHE
An aching tooth is perhaps the most common dental emergency, and
is certainly one of the most unpleasant.The usual cause is tooth decay,
but there are so many other possibilities that only your dentist, trained
to analyze the entire situation, may be able to diagnose and relieve it.
The pain will sometimes be reduced or temporarily resolved by
ingesting an analgesics like an aspirin. Ice may give relief, but inflamed
tooth nerves sometimes don't respond to it, and occasionally become
even more painful. Don't apply a hot pack. Toothache often involves a
bacterial infection, and heat can cause it to spread. Don't put an
aspirin tablet against the tooth or gum. The acid can burn the soft
tissues of the mouth, Until you can get treatment, try not to chew on
the tooth, and avoid hot or sweet foods.
Remember, pain is the body's way of giving you a message. It's a
signal that something ,is wrong. Even if the toothache goes away, you
should go to your dentist for an examination as soon as possible.

Why I left my childhood church


Pope Benedict XVI


sends blessings


REV. SUSANNE NAZIAN
REGIONN COLUMNIST


I was born into a Southern
Baptist family, which meant I
was also born into the church
in our rural community: the
Calvary Baptist Church of
Chester, S.C. I left home at 17
to attend college, where I
majored in Religious Studies. I
attended the First Baptist
Church in my college city, and
found it to be quite different
from the one I'd attended
before. There was a Sunday
school class led by a history
professor at the college,
whose 'avocation was moral
philosophy. In it, I found a
forum for free discussion of
religious questions, and a
group of young people who
were compatible with my
interests. It served as a great
transition for a thinking
young person into a more
mature faith. I
I used to go home some
weekends to attend the old'
church. The pastor was a
good man, I think, but one
whose theological and moral
views were at odds with mine.
They took the Bible very liter-
ally in that church, and I dis-
covered one Sunday that we
had grown too far apart.
Taking the Bible too literal-
ly can be a dangerous thing.
I've found that it is, always
subject to interpretation. It
was even subject to interpre-
tation then, by this Baptist
preacher. Sometimes, they
got it wrong.


South Carolina in those
days was a bastion of racial
hatred. My family just didn't
live that way, even though
they were known to spout the
rhetoric. I watched my moth-
er as she cared for our neigh-
bors when they were ill -
black, white it didn't make
anr difference; if they were
sick, she nursed them. I
watched my father. He was a
farmer and if the black'
farmer's crops didn't come in
and his did, he saw to it that
they had fresh vegetables. I
grew up knowing that no
matter what the culture said,
people were people no matter
what color or how poor or any
other difference one might
imagine. When our high
school integrated, I was one
of the very few who support-
ed it.
I believed that the love my
church taught extended to
everyone. I might have stayed
in that church, at least longer
than I did, if I'd believed they
stood -on that principle.- I
never thought theology mat-
tered as much as community
and these people were the
ones who mattered tome.
But, that was not to be.
One of those times when Ij
returned to the old home
church was in the midst of the
civil rights struggle. I was an
admirer of such men as
Bobby Kennedy and Martin
Luther King Jr. The sermon
began with scriptural quotes,
mostly from the Old Test-
ament. As I began to suspect
where this sermon was going,
I became increasingly un-
comfortable. So when that
preacher got up and said that
we did not and. never would
welcome black people into
our church, and used the
scripture to back up his opin-
ion, I got up in the middle of
the sermon and walked out. I
was never to return to a
Baptist church.


My brother and sister-in-
law remained there, and then
later at the First Baptist. Over
the years, the scripture that
had been interpreted -as for-,
bidding the mixing of the
races was reinterpreted. Now
it was good to love your
African-American neighbor
and welcome him to worship
with you.
Then, it was time for an-
other group on which to focus
people's fears. The Bible could
be used to condemn homo-
sexuals. Somebody could
quote Leviticus at me and tell
me my homosexual friends
were an abomination. I knew
better then, and I know better
now. Much like my parents
loved their neighbors, people
whom they knew, despite
what the church or the cul-
ture told them, I knew people
who were homosexuals.
These people lived good lives,
were professionals or going to
be, dated, loved and became
couples like anyone else.
They loved their church; it
was just that their church did-
n't love them.
For many years, I was an
outcast from my church. The
gospel they had taught me
didn't jibe with the treatment
I saw extended to those who
were my neighbors. In my
adult years, 19 years after I left
the Baptist church, I found a
church whose history went
back as many years as the
Baptists. The difference was
that the gospel of love extend-
ed to all who would commit
to making it real and vital in
this world. They demanded of
me that I hang on to the
things I really believed, and
that I search for more that I
grow in faith. I was at home
the very first day I went to a
Unitarian Universalist Church.
Two years later, I finally
answered the call to ministry

Please see NAZIAN, 78


4f

Leading the way

NA21AN from Page 6B


that had first come when I
was -16 at a Baptist Con-
ference Center. I entered a
Unitarian Universalist semi-
nary,' and at age of 47 was
ordained to the ministry.
I understand much more
now. I value the Baptists for
what they taught me about
the value of religious conimu-
nity, the loving kindness that I
now believe is the. birthright
of every human being, and
the teachings of Jesus that
love of neighbors is the most
important of religion's com-
mandments. I value the other
faiths that have informed my
thinking and convinced me
that there is a wideness in
God's love and mercy that
extends to all who live.
I .am grateful to Unitarian
Universalism, which has
given voice to all I believe and
know. I am grateful to have a
place where I can question,
learn and grow in love. I now
know through the study of
psychology, medical science,
sociology and the sacred
books of many traditions that
homosexuals are born, not
made, and that part of realiz-
ing their full humanity is to be
known, accepted and loved in
their fullness as human be-


ings.
Reconciliation to the
church is difficult for homo-
sexuals, because the church
has done harm to them. They
have been told that who they
are and who they love is
wrong, sinful. Is there any
wonder so many are without
church affiliations? I am
proud of being part of a
church that welcomes homo-
sexuals as God's children. I
am proud of being part of a
church that works on its prej-
udices, and confronts its
shortcomings. Frequently in
our services we recite an affir-
mation: "Love is the doctrine
of this church, the quest for
truth its sacrament, and ser-
vice is its prayer."
May it be so for all of us as
we try to make sense of this
world.

Rev. Susanne Nazian is
minister of the Unitarian
Universalist Congregation of
Venice. The church began an
educational and discussion
series on homosexuality Nov.
1. This series is free and open
to the public. More informa-
tion can be obtained by e-
mailing the church at
uucov@aol.com.

KIM COOL
FEATURES EDITOR


Local artist David Landry,
well-known for his palm tree
carvings, has a new challenge
- pancreatic cancer. For
years, Landry donated exam-
ples of his artwork to many
local organizations. Saturday,
Nov. 12, members of those
groups and his friends will
turn the tables on him. They
are holding a fundraising
event at the Nokomis Com-
munity Center, 234 Nippino
Trail, 2-9 p.m. At 2 p.m. there
will be a video presentation
called "Hearts of Palm," which
will showcase the artist and
his work. At 4 p.m. there will
be a live auction featuring art-
work by Landry and other
area artists. At 5 p.m. there
will be a raffle and door
prizes. From 6 to 9 p.m., a
potluck luau, drumming and
entertainment is scheduled.


In addition to attending
the event and purchasing art-
work, everyone can help b y
donating items for the auc-
tion, raffle and door prizes.
Also needed are volunteers,
potluck dishes, tables, chairs
and such things as picnic
items.
For more information or to
volunteer your time, call
Germaine Delaney at (941)
480-9029 or e-mail tangotikis
@aol.com.
There is more information
about the event at David
Landry.com.

Sarasota Opera honors the

patron and special friend, Ilse
Kern, with a musical celebra-

the Sarasota Opera House on
Nov. 14 at 7 p.m. Featuring
singers from the Sarasota
Opera, this memorial concert
will include spoken tributes
from her friends and col-
leagues.
Kern, who passed away on
July 8 from heart failure, was
on' the Board of Trustees of
Sarasota Opera for more than
a decade. She also served as
chair of the audit committee
and on the executive and
finance committees and was
very active in marketing and
public relations efforts for the

Salmen High School, a
public high school in Slidell,
La., near Lake Pontchartrain
and 20 miles north of New


tew
C'T'FAY'


Orleans, was hit hard by Hur-
ricane Katrina. All the build-
ings were completely covered
by a 15-foot tidal surge. Only
the rooftops were left visible.
Nothing in the library is sal-
vageable. The school is cur-
rently sharing facilities with
another school and hopes to
begin the rebuilding process
after the winter holidays. Iii
addition to the building dam-
age, the 11,000-volume
school library was a total loss.
Southern Association Accred-
itation standards require a
school library to contain at
least 10 books per registered
student, hence the need to
replace the library as soon as
possible. If you have books
appropriate to a high school
library, including reference
works plus fiction or non-fic-
tion, preferably' library-
bound, send them to Li-
brarian Nancy McKee, Sal-
men High School, 100 Pan-
ther Drive, Slidell, LA 70461.
For more information, call
(985) 649-6400 (Northshore
High) or (985) 649-5987 (the
library). Send the books at
book rate. :

The Players Theatre holds
auditions for the final three
shows of its 76th Broadway
musical season on Nov. 19.
The directors of "The Most
Happy Fella," "Man of La
Mancha" and Stephen Sond-
heim's review, "Putting It


Together" will audition act-
ors, singers and dancers to fill
the dozens of roles available
for the 2006 shows.
Those wishing to audition
should prepare a one-minute
song and one-minute mono-
logue of their choice. Perusal
copies of each script are avail-
able at the theater for reading.
Copies of "sides" may be
picked up one week prior to
the audition. Those interested
should call The Players
Theatre administrative offices
at 365-2494 to schedule a spe-'
cific time slot. Time slots
begin at noon Saturday, Nov.
19. "The Most Happy Fella'
plays Jan. 12-29; "Man of La
Mancha" runs Feb. 23-March
12; and "Putting It Together"
will be produced March 30-

Members of the Pinebrook
South Camera Club will,
exhibit framed prints .that
demonstrate the versatility
and capabilities of digital
technology in an exhibit that
opens with a reception, 5-6:
p.m., Tuesday, Nov. 15, at the
Manatee Community College
(MCC) Venice Fine Art Gallery,
8000 South Tamiami Trail. The
reception is free and open to
. the public. Refreshments will
be served.
The club was founded by
Lowell Wentworth in 1991 for
residents of the Pinebrook
subdivision in Venice and
others who share a love of
photography. All members
use digital, single lens, reflex
cameras and a variety of lens-
es and do their own matting.
Approximately 60 prints will
be displayed in the MCC
exhibit, including panoramas

PHOTO COURTESY OF HUMANE SOCIETY OF SARASOTA COUNTY
Oreo is a 6-month-old, female lab mix. She has beautiful gold-
en eyes and an adoring gaze. Her former owner described her
as friendly, affectionate and gentle. Oreo has lived with kids
older than 6 and with dogs, and she loves belly rubs. She
knows the sit, stay and down commands, even though she
hasn't had formal training. This is a really sweet dog who
would be a great playmate. Can you give Oreo a home? Visit
the Humane Society of Sarasota County at 2331:15th St.,
Sarasota, call 955-4131, or visit hssc.org.

BY DR. MARLY BECKER.
GUEST WRITER

In libraries and classrooms
in over 40 states, children who
are afraid to read to adults
build their confidence and
reading skills by reading to
friendly, furry, nonjudgmen-
tal four-legged friends.-
Also known as Reading
Education Assistance Dogs
(READ), Intermountain Ther-
apy Animals in Salt Lake City,
Utah, started the program in
1999 and has seen it grow to
include more than 350 READ
teams across the U.S. accord-
ing to Veterinary Economics
magazine.
A handler sits nearby dur-
ing the 30-minute reading
session.
A combination reading
coach. and dog trainer, the
trained handler interfaces


between the child and the
pet, keeping the pet focused
on the child's face or the
book.
Or, the handler might ask
the child to repeat or explain
a word that the dog doesn't
understand.
While it sounds unusual
and cute, university studies
have shown the READ pro-
gram really works with some
children jumping several
grade levels in reading profi-
ciency in just a few weeks.
Dr. Marty Becker is coau-
thor of "Chicken Soup For
The Horse Lover's Soul" and a
popular veterinary contribu-
tor forABC TV's "Good
MorningAmerica."Write to
him in care of Knight
Ridder/Tribune News Service,
700 12th St. NE, STE 1000,
Washington, DC20005.

A Big charity event


BY CHRISTINA ELWELL
STAFF WRITER

The Big Brothers Big Sisters
of the Sun Coast's 15th Annu-
al Lively Arts and Comedy
Auction was held on Saturday,
Nov. 5. Proceeds from the
event will support the mentor-
ing programs held by Big
Brothers Big Sisters.
A large crowd gathered at
the Plantation Golf & Country
Club, where the charity event
took place. Each of them at-
tended to show support for
BBBS.
Bob and Alex Metzger were


one couple that came to sup-
port the cause. Bob Metzger is
on the board of BBBS and will
soon be receiving a little
brother to mentor.
"He's taking over my
mother's little brother," Alex
Metzger explained.
Co-chairs of the event
were Melissa Caldwell and
Michele Stephan. Melissa
Caldwell's mother, Annette
Caldwell, was one of the
many event coordinators.
"We've been working on
this for months. We have
wonderful art and gifts from
merchants," Annette Caldwell


Fred Schuchert entertained the guests at the BBBS event with
his fantastic piano skills..


at 720 Alligator Drive. BYOB;
50/50 raffle. Tickets are $6.
ARC mobile clinic
The Animal Rescue Coalition
mobile clinic offers low- or
no-cost spays and neuters
for pets of income-eligible
families at sites throughout
Sarasota County. Appoint-
ments required by calling
957-1955, Ext. 5.
Upcoming sites include:
Friday, Nov. 18, at Jacaranda
Plaza, Kmart parking lot,
Venice; other sites in Sarasota
and North.Port.

Blvd., Venice. Residents or

497-7366.
Winzerfest
The, German American
Friendship Club's Winzerfest
takes place at 7 p.m., Satur-
day, Nov. 19, at the South
Venice Yacht Club, 4425
Yacht Club Drive. Music by


said.
Thesd donations ranged
from a Gund Living Puppet
donated by Cuddlebugs to
cases of wine donated by
Devine's Wine Bar. 50/50 raffle
tickets were sold by Suntrust
Bank's Janice Lott and Co-
lonial Bank's Liz Crapet.
After a silent auction and
hors d'oeuvres, a live auction
and comedy show was put on
by Ken Sons and Les McCurdy.
Items for bid in the live
auction included a Lake
Tahoe Escape donated by
John Lee, a Walt Disney Park
Adventure for four donated
by Keys-Caldwell and WVal
Disney World and a Star
pendant diamond necklace
donated by Reflections in
Gold. And that's just to name
a few of the 13 items.
Once the auction was
complete, Sons and McCurdy
asked the auction audience
who would be willing to do-
nate an extra $50 to BBBS.
Several people were happy to
raise their paddle.
For more information
about Big Brothers Big Sisters
visit their Web site at bbbsa.
org.

REAL ESTATE CLASSIFIED


Canal-side home is a tropical oasis


From the covered boat davit behind the house, there is a


lengthy canal view.

BY KIM COOL
FEATURES EDITOR


Location is everything.
In Florida, the most desir-
able (that is valuable) loca-
tions are on the water. Histor-
ically, sites on canals or along
branches of the Intracoastal
Waterway or along rivers such
as thdie Myakka, have fetched
higher prices than lots direct-
ly on the Gulf of Mexico.
New on the market is a
Port Charlotte house on one
of the widest deep-water ca-
nals in the Sarasota or Char-
lotte county area, with
bridgeless-access to the My-
akka River, which leads to the
Gulf of Mexico, via a sailboat-
frieandly bridge at El Jobean.
At the back of the lot is a new
dock with covered slip and


newer davit to hoist power
boats out of the water. Sail-
boaters will appreciate the
deep water and the 115-foot-
long seawall.
In addition to the extra
long new dock, the lot is
wider than most, measuring
115 by 132 feet. Those dimen-
sions allowed the prior resi-
dent space on which to create
a tree-shaded area in which
to hang a hammock. Other
areas of tropical plantings
provide privacy to the caged
pool while not obstructing
the view of the canal behind
the house.
The home is a classic split
plan with two bedrooms and
two baths, which work like
two separate master suites.
The second suite features a
shower with a sliding door to


*"... .:'" '.

An etched-glass heron is a decorative element in the breakfast
nook.


an outdoor garden area sur-
rounded by a stuccoed wall
for privacy.
The master suite has two
walk-in closets, matching his
and hers vanities in the bath
area and a 15-foot by 20-foot
bedroom with French doors,
which open, to the inground
pool area.
Stained beams accent the
living room's cathedral ceil-
ing, which is further embell-
ished with a fan featuring
palm-frond shaped fan
blades.
Neutral colors predomi-
nate throughout the house
and include off-white berber-
'type carpeting and tile floor-
ing and white walls. Kitchen
counters are a soft gray
against white recess-panel
doors and white appliances.


An etched heron decorates a
sliding glass door at the back
of the kitchen's eatng space.
Located at 13441 Palau
Circle, the house has been
listed for $479,900 by Sellstate'
Realtor Dennis Gardner of the
SW Florida New Homes,
Realty, Team. Take U.S. 41
south to Port Charlotte and
State Road 776 west to Jacobs
Road. Turn right. Palau willbe
!1.5 miles ahead on the left.
There will be an open
house Saturday, Nov. 12,
noon-4 p.m. For an appoint-
ment to see the house at
another time, call Gardner at
(800) 965-0910 or (239) 470-
9200.
For additional information
on this and other properties,
visit SWFloridaNewHomes
.Info.

A koi pond is located at the home's front entrance.
